<div id="review_265438637" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/265438637" />
    <a title="Jenna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/82453-jenna"><img alt="Jenna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601135180p2/82453.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/265438637?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenna" href="/user/show/82453-jenna">Jenna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/82453-jenna?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/82453-jenna?shelf=fairytalesque">fairytalesque</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/82453-jenna?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer265438637" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5514395623548176078">I've been thinking quite a bit about this book lately. I loved it when I first read it as a fourteen-year-old. (This was not long after I read <i>Villette</i> by Charlotte Bronte and <i>Rebecca</i> by Daphne du Maurier, two amazing novels that opened my eyes to the manifold benefits of giving a book a strong female first-person narrator.)<br/><br/>McKillip is not for everybody: her writing is highly stylized, intensely florid, and stubbornly pre-Raphaelite in its aesthetics. She uses phrases like "He rode a horse the c</span>
  <span id="freeText5514395623548176078" style="display:none">I've been thinking quite a bit about this book lately. I loved it when I first read it as a fourteen-year-old. (This was not long after I read <i>Villette</i> by Charlotte Bronte and <i>Rebecca</i> by Daphne du Maurier, two amazing novels that opened my eyes to the manifold benefits of giving a book a strong female first-person narrator.)<br /><br />McKillip is not for everybody: her writing is highly stylized, intensely florid, and stubbornly pre-Raphaelite in its aesthetics. She uses phrases like "He rode a horse the color of buttermilk...": i.e., the kind of phrases that are drummed out of today's aspiring authors in university writing workshops throughout the country.<br /><br />All this raises the suspicion that McKillip is self-taught, an idiosyncratic artist who sprang out from underneath a mossy boulder somewhere, deliciously naive and miraculously untouched by the ubiquitous influence of literary realism.<br /><br /><i>Winter Rose</i>'s main influences are: the Scottish folk ballad "Tam Lin," the Grimm Brothers' fairy tale "Snow White and Rose Red," and Christina Rossetti's bizarro long poem "Goblin Market" (1862). This 1997 novel's heroine is the younger of two sisters, a tomboyish misfit who enjoys climbing trees and scouring the underbrush for medicinal herbs. The two sisters fall obsessively in love with the same man, but the man only reciprocates the older sister's feelings. Teenage girls with strong emotions will find the experience of reading <i>Winter Rose</i> to be a vindication of sorts, for the book depicts how an agonizingly one-sided love can be a preternaturally powerful force <i>despite its one-sidedness</i>: ultimately, the tomboy's love proves powerful enough to save the life of her beloved, to solve an ancient murder mystery, and to defeat a demonic fairy queen. YA fantasy at its best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5514395623548176078" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_509483540" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/509483540" />
    <a title="Juushika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/616321-juushika"><img alt="Juushika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541586729p2/616321.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/509483540?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Juushika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Juushika" href="/user/show/616321-juushika">Juushika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=status-borrowed">status-borrowed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=genre-fantasy">genre-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=subgenre-retelling">subgenre-retelling</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer509483540" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2357810127476047991">When Corbet Lynn comes to reclaim the dilapidated Lynn Hall, the wild Rois--given to foraging barefoot in the woods--becomes obsessed with the secrets and curses of his past. <i>Winter Rose</i> is a Tam Lin retelling at its best: it harvests some aspects (from Tam Lin and other tales) and discards others while maintaining the emotional and symbolic essence of the source material; it then weaves an entire tale around that skeleton, creating a vivid setting and cast without losing the story's magic. Inde</span>
  <span id="freeText2357810127476047991" style="display:none">When Corbet Lynn comes to reclaim the dilapidated Lynn Hall, the wild Rois--given to foraging barefoot in the woods--becomes obsessed with the secrets and curses of his past. <i>Winter Rose</i> is a Tam Lin retelling at its best: it harvests some aspects (from Tam Lin and other tales) and discards others while maintaining the emotional and symbolic essence of the source material; it then weaves an entire tale around that skeleton, creating a vivid setting and cast without losing the story's magic. Indeed, McKillip's prose is alive with it--her voice is jewel-toned and embroidered, rich with imagery and distinctly magical; it's reminiscent of McKinley but, while occasionally too dreamlike, is not prone to McKinley's frothy atmosphere. The story that grows from it is a tapestry of symbols, a complete internal mythology fueled by resonant emotion and character agency. In a word, it's beautiful. At times it threatens to run off with itself, and the characters and rural setting lean towards banality, but <i>Winter Rose</i> is nonetheless the best of what symbolism can be: entwined images of captivating beauty, startlingly precise and meaningful. This was my introduction to McKillip, and I couldn't ask for better. I recommended it to the interested: if you're here for imagery, McKillip satisfies; if not, the style may wear thin.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2357810127476047991" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vwGLSu6yYv+ZF4Y9KLoHz1l2LH4zSK8TATGeyxsdjgbNjN9owGSC+jRcEq/Zf6vneGgpggx7a5Vz/w07SlTFqA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2eg5xZiihmijSJLTAeFSNZybtF7kDrCJXo2d9L4JxD6rZW3ntnRmbQ4DBkHwJP4dvYWxots9dA8sQw4E70CPkA==')})
  }

Cold as winter, she has only one passion—for the mysterious hunter who courts the outlawed old gods of the woodland. Coira is the princess raised in the shadow of her mother’s hatred. Avoided by both her parents and half forgotten by her father’s court, she grows into womanhood alone . . . until the mirror speaks, and blood is spilled, and the forest claims her.The tragic myth of the goddess Demeter and her daughter, Persephone, stolen by the king of the underworld, is woven together with the tale of Snow White to create a powerful story of mothers and daughters and the blood that binds them together, for good or ill.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/25.Patricia_A_McKillip">About Patricia A. McKillip</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7926359810278302711">Patricia Anne McKillip is an American author of fantasy and science fiction novels, distinguished by lyrical, delicate prose and careful attention to detail and characterization. She is a past winner of the World Fantasy Award and Locus Award, and she lives in Oregon. Most of her recent novels have cover paintings by Kinuko Y. Craft. She is married to David Lunde, a poet.<br/><br/>According to Fantasy Book </span>
  <span id="freeText7926359810278302711" style="display:none">Patricia Anne McKillip is an American author of fantasy and science fiction novels, distinguished by lyrical, delicate prose and careful attention to detail and characterization. She is a past winner of the World Fantasy Award and Locus Award, and she lives in Oregon. Most of her recent novels have cover paintings by Kinuko Y. Craft. She is married to David Lunde, a poet.<br /><br />According to Fantasy Book Review, Patricia McKillip grew up in Oregon, England, and Germany, and received a Bachelor of Arts (English) in 1971 and a Master of Arts in 1973 from San Jose State University.<br /><br />McKillip's stories usually take place in a setting similar to the Middle Ages. There are forests, castles, and lords or kings, minstrels, tinkers and wizards. Her writing usually puts her characters in situations involving mysterious powers that they don't understand. Many of her characters aren't even sure of their own ancestry. Music often plays an important role. Love between family members is also important in McKillip's writing, although members of her families often disagree.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7926359810278302711" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
